    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    (function(w, d) {

        w.ra21Host = 'https://wayf.springernature.com';
        var moduleSupport = (function() {
            return 'noModule' in d.createElement('script');
        })();

        if (w.config.mustardcut === true) {
            w.loader = {
                index: 0,
                registered: [],
                scripts: [
                    
                        {src: '/static/js/global-article-es6-bundle-3c95024fee.js', test: 'global-article-js', module: true},
                        {src: '/static/js/global-article-es5-bundle-2038893a49.js', test: 'global-article-js', nomodule: true},

                ].filter(function (s) {
                    if (s.src === null) return false;
                    if (moduleSupport && s.nomodule) return false;
                    return !(!moduleSupport && s.module);
                }),

                register: function (value) {
                    this.registered.push(value);
                },

                ready: function () {
                    if (this.registered.length === this.scripts.length) {
                        this.registered.forEach(function (fn) {
                            if (typeof fn === 'function') {
                                setTimeout(fn, 0); 
                            }
                        });
                        this.ready = function () {};
                    }
                },

                insert: function (s) {
                    var t = d.getElementById('js-position' + this.index);
                    if (t && t.insertAdjacentElement) {
                        t.insertAdjacentElement('afterend', s);
                    } else {
                        d.head.appendChild(s);
                    }
                    ++this.index;
                },

                createScript: function (script, beforeLoad) {
                    var s = d.createElement('script');
                    s.id = 'js-position' + (this.index + 1);
                    s.setAttribute('data-test', script.test);
                    if (beforeLoad) {
                        s.defer = 'defer';
                        s.onload = function () {
                            if (script.noinit) {
                                loader.register(true);
                            }
                            if (d.readyState === 'interactive' || d.readyState === 'complete') {
                                loader.ready();
                            }
                        };
                    } else {
                        s.async = 'async';
                    }
                    s.src = script.src;
                    return s;
                },

                init: function () {
                    this.scripts.forEach(function (s) {
                        loader.insert(loader.createScript(s, true));
                    });

                    d.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
                        loader.ready();
                        var conditionalScripts;

    <script type="application/ld+json">{"mainEntity":{"headline":"Superextensive electrical power from a quantum battery","description":"Superextensivity, where the response of a physical system scales super-linearly with size, originates from collective quantum effects and provides a promising route to augment next-generation quantum technologies. While recent work has demonstrated superextensive behaviour in the coherent dynamics of quantum systems, these effects typically occur on short timescales, prohibiting their practical utility. In contrast, triggering steady-state superextensive effects in, for example, a generated electric current, remains unexplored despite the immediate impact on photovoltaic technologies. Here, we utilise a microcavity quantum battery as an experimental platform that superextensively captures light energy and converts it to an electric current via the incorporation of charge transport layers into the resonant microcavity. This architecture enables, for the first time, a complete quantum battery charge-discharge cycle. We demonstrate that strong light–matter coupling induced by the microcavity leads to superextensive scaling of the steady-state electrical discharging power under low-intensity, incoherent illumination. Our results provide the first experimental demonstration of superextensive light-to-charge conversion in steady-state, highlighting the feasibility of leveraging strong light–matter coupling for enhanced energy harvesting under low-light conditions.","datePublished":"2026-03-13T00:00:00Z","dateModified":"2026-03-13T00:00:00Z","pageStart":"1","pageEnd":"11","license":"http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/","sameAs":"https://doi.org/10.1038/s41377-026-02240-6","keywords":["Photonic devices","Polaritons","Optics","Lasers","Photonics","Optical Devices","Microwaves","RF and Optical Engineering","Optical and Electronic Materials"],"image":["https://media.springernature.com/lw1200/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41377-026-02240-6/MediaObjects/41377_2026_2240_Fig1_HTML.png","https://media.springernature.com/lw1200/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41377-026-02240-6/MediaObjects/41377_2026_2240_Fig2_HTML.png","https://media.springernature.com/lw1200/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41377-026-02240-6/MediaObjects/41377_2026_2240_Fig3_HTML.png","https://media.springernature.com/lw1200/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41377-026-02240-6/MediaObjects/41377_2026_2240_Fig4_HTML.png"],"isPartOf":{"name":"Light: Science & Applications","issn":["2047-7538"],"volumeNumber":"15","@type":["Periodical","PublicationVolume"]},"publisher":{"name":"Nature Publishing Group UK","logo":{"url":"https://www.springernature.com/app-sn/public/images/logo-springernature.png","@type":"ImageObject"},"@type":"Organization"},"author":[{"name":"Kieran Hymas","url":"http://orcid.org/0000-0003-1761-4298","affiliation":[{"name":"Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation (CSIRO)","address":{"name":"Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation (CSIRO), Clayton, Australia","@type":"PostalAddress"},"@type":"Organization"}],"@type":"Person"},{"name":"Jack B.     <meta name="dc.description" content="Superextensivity, where the response of a physical system scales super-linearly with size, originates from collective quantum effects and provides a promising route to augment next-generation quantum technologies. While recent work has demonstrated superextensive behaviour in the coherent dynamics of quantum systems, these effects typically occur on short timescales, prohibiting their practical utility. In contrast, triggering steady-state superextensive effects in, for example, a generated electric current, remains unexplored despite the immediate impact on photovoltaic technologies. Here, we utilise a microcavity quantum battery as an experimental platform that superextensively captures light energy and converts it to an electric current via the incorporation of charge transport layers into the resonant microcavity. This architecture enables, for the first time, a complete quantum battery charge-discharge cycle. We demonstrate that strong light&#8211;matter coupling induced by the microcavity leads to superextensive scaling of the steady-state electrical discharging power under low-intensity, incoherent illumination. Our results provide the first experimental demonstration of superextensive light-to-charge conversion in steady-state, highlighting the feasibility of leveraging strong light&#8211;matter coupling for enhanced energy harvesting under low-light conditions."/>

        </nav>

        <div class="c-article-body">
            <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Superextensivity, where the response of a physical system scales super-linearly with size, originates from collective quantum effects and provides a promising route to augment next-generation quantum technologies. While recent work has demonstrated superextensive behaviour in the coherent dynamics of quantum systems, these effects typically occur on short timescales, prohibiting their practical utility. In contrast, triggering steady-state superextensive effects in, for example, a generated electric current, remains unexplored despite the immediate impact on photovoltaic technologies. Here, we utilise a microcavity quantum battery as an experimental platform that superextensively captures light energy and converts it to an electric current via the incorporation of charge transport layers into the resonant microcavity. This architecture enables, for the first time, a complete quantum battery charge-discharge cycle. We demonstrate that strong light–matter coupling induced by the microcavity leads to superextensive scaling of the steady-state electrical discharging power under low-intensity, incoherent illumination. Our results provide the first experimental demonstration of superextensive light-to-charge conversion in steady-state, highlighting the feasibility of leveraging strong light–matter coupling for enhanced energy harvesting under low-light conditions.</p></div></div></section>

                <div class="main-content">
                    
                        <section data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The deployment of quantum technologies, with unique and often counterintuitive advantages over their classical counterparts, presents an exciting prospect for both commercial applications and the advancement of foundational quantum physics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dowling, J. P. &amp; Milburn, G. J. Quantum technology: the second quantum revolution. Philos. Trans. R. Soc. A 361, 1655–1674 (2003)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e504">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="O’brien, J. L., Furusawa, A. &amp; Vučković, J. Photonic quantum technologies. Nat. Photonics 3, 687–695 (2009)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e504_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Pelucchi, E. et al. The potential and global outlook of integrated photonics for quantum technologies. Nat. Rev. Phys. 4, 194–208 (2022)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e507">3</a></sup>. While quantum computing has historically garnered the most attention, other prospective quantum technologies are now emerging with distinct quantum advantages, such as quantum sensors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Degen, C. L., Reinhard, F. &amp; Cappellaro, P. Quantum sensing. Rev. Mod. Phys. 89, 035002 (2017)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e511">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Pirandola, S. et al. Advances in photonic quantum sensing. Nat. Photonics 12, 724–733 (2018)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e511_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Crawford, S. E. et al. Quantum sensing for energy applications: review and perspective. Adv. Quantum Technol. 4, 2100049 (2021)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e514">6</a></sup>, quantum simulators<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Daley, A. J. et al. Practical quantum advantage in quantum simulation. Nature 607, 667–676 (2022)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e518">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Cornish, S. L., Tarbutt, M. R. &amp; Hazzard, K. R. A. Quantum computation and quantum simulation with ultracold molecules. Nat. Phys. 20, 730–740 (2024)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e521">8</a></sup> and quantum batteries (QBs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Alicki, R. &amp; Fannes, M. Entanglement boost for extractable work from ensembles of quantum batteries. Phys. Rev. E 87, 042123 (2013)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e525">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Quach, J. Q., Cerullo, G. &amp; Virgili, T. Quantum batteries: the future of energy storage? Joule 7, 2195–2200 (2023)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e525_1">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Campaioli, F. et al. Colloquium: quantum batteries. Rev. Mod. Phys. 96, 031001 (2024)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e528">11</a></sup>. In each embodiment, the quantum advantage stems from entanglement. In quantum batteries, entanglement serves to minimise the number of traversed states during charging<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Hovhannisyan, K. V. et al. Entanglement generation is not necessary for optimal work extraction. Phys. Rev. Lett. 111, 240401 (2013)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e532">12</a></sup>, or trigger collective effects that increase the effective coupling between the battery and its energy source<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ferraro, D. et al. High-power collective charging of a solid-state quantum battery. Phys. Rev. Lett. 120, 117702 (2018)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e537">13</a></sup>. Consequently, they are theorised to exhibit exotic properties such as a charging power that scales faster than the battery capacity (i.e., superextensively)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Alicki, R. &amp; Fannes, M. Entanglement boost for extractable work from ensembles of quantum batteries. Phys. Rev. E 87, 042123 (2013)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e541">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Hovhannisyan, K. V. et al. Entanglement generation is not necessary for optimal work extraction. Phys. Rev. Lett. 111, 240401 (2013)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e544">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Binder, F. C. et al. Quantacell: powerful charging of quantum batteries. N. J. Phys. 17, 075015 (2015)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e547">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Campaioli, F. et al. Enhancing the charging power of quantum batteries. Phys. Rev. Lett. 118, 150601 (2017)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e550">15</a></sup> as well as increased bounds on energy density<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Hotta, M. &amp; Kazuki, I. Exceeding the maximum classical energy density in fully charged quantum batteries. Quantum Inf. Process. 24, 186 (2025)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e554">16</a></sup> and storage lifetime<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Mohan, B. &amp; Pati, A. K. Reverse quantum speed limit: how slowly a quantum battery can discharge. Phys. Rev. A 104, 042209 (2021)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e558">17</a></sup>. Although there is a large body of literature on the theory of quantum batteries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Le, T. P. et al. Spin-chain model of a many-body quantum battery. Phys. Rev. A 97, 022106 (2018)." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Andolina, G. M. et al. Extractable work, the role of correlations, and asymptotic freedom in quantum batteries. Phys. Rev. Lett. 122, 047702 (2019)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562_1">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, Y. Y. et al. Powerful harmonic charging in a quantum battery. Phys. Rev. E 99, 052106 (2019)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562_2">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Barra, F. Dissipative charging of a quantum battery. Phys. Rev. Lett. 122, 210601 (2019)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562_3">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, J. J., Segal, D. &amp; Hanna, G. Loss-free excitonic quantum battery. J. Phys. Chem. C 123, 18303–18314 (2019)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562_4">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Santos, A. C. et al. Stable adiabatic quantum batteries. Phys. Rev. E 100, 032107 (2019)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562_5">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rossini, D., Andolina, G. M. &amp; Polini, M. Many-body localized quantum batteries. Phys. Rev. B 100, 115142 (2019)." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562_6">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Monsel, J. et al. The energetic cost of work extraction. Phys. Rev. Lett. 124, 130601 (2020)." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562_7">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Julià-Farré, S. et al. Bounds on the capacity and power of quantum batteries. Phys. Rev. Res. 2, 023113 (2020)." href="#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562_8">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Crescente, A. et al. Charging and energy fluctuations of a driven quantum battery. N. J. Phys. 22, 063057 (2020)." href="#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562_9">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Quach, J. Q. &amp; Munro, W. J. Using dark states to charge and stabilize open quantum batteries. Phys. Rev. Appl. 14, 024092 (2020)." href="#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562_10">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rossini, D. et al. Quantum advantage in the charging process of Sachdev-Ye-Kitaev batteries. Phys. Rev. Lett. 125, 236402 (2020)." href="#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562_11">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Crescente, A. et al. Ultrafast charging in a two-photon Dicke quantum battery. Phys. Rev. B 102, 245407 (2020)." href="#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562_12">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Seah, S. et al. Quantum speed-up in collisional battery charging. Phys. Rev. Lett. 127, 100601 (2021)." href="#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562_13">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ghosh, S. et al. Fast charging of a quantum battery assisted by noise. Phys. Rev. A 104, 032207 (2021)." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562_14">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, J. X. et al. Entanglement and work extraction in the central-spin quantum battery. Phys. Rev. B 104, 245418 (2021)." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562_15">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Auffèves, A. Quantum technologies need a quantum energy initiative. PRX Quantum 3, 020101 (2022)." href="#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562_16">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Carrasco, J. et al. Collective enhancement in dissipative quantum batteries. Phys. Rev. E 105, 064119 (2022)." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e562_17">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Catalano, A. G. et al. Frustrating quantum batteries. PRX Quantum 5, 030319 (2024)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e565">36</a></sup> very few experiments exist<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yang, D. et al. Realization of superabsorption by time reversal of superradiance. Nat. Photonics 15, 272–276 (2021)." href="#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e569">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Quach, J. Q. et al. Superabsorption in an organic microcavity: toward a quantum battery. Sci. Adv. 8, eabk3160 (2022)." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e569_1">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hu, C. K. et al. Optimal charging of a superconducting quantum battery. Quantum Sci. Technol. 7, 045018 (2022)." href="#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e569_2">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gemme, G. et al. IBM quantum platforms: a quantum battery perspective. Batteries 8, 43 (2022)." href="#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e569_3">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Joshi, J. &amp; Mahesh, T. S. Experimental investigation of a quantum battery using star-topology NMR spin systems. Phys. Rev. A 106, 042601 (2022)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e572">41</a></sup>. These experiments investigated isolated properties of the quantum battery only, and none demonstrated a fully functioning quantum battery.</p><p>Quantum batteries are an experimental testbed for thermodynamics beyond the classical limit, where bounds on energy transfer and heat generation may be overcome by quantum correlations and quantum fluctuations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Camposeo, A. et al. Quantum batteries: a materials science perspective. Adv. Mater. 37, 2415073 (2025)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e579">42</a></sup>. Superabsorption of energy from light, the time-reversal of superradiance, is an example of faster-than-classical energy-transfer that was recently demonstrated in quantum batteries comprised of cold-atoms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Yang, D. et al. Realization of superabsorption by time reversal of superradiance. Nat. Photonics 15, 272–276 (2021)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e583">37</a></sup> as well as organic molecules in an optical cavity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Quach, J. Q. et al. Superabsorption in an organic microcavity: toward a quantum battery. Sci. Adv. 8, eabk3160 (2022)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e587">38</a></sup>. In the optical microcavity, superabsorption was facilitated by molecular correlations triggered by a collective coupling to the fundamental mode of the confined electromagnetic field. For <span class="mathjax-tex">\(N\)</span> molecules, this coupling scaled as <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\sqrt{N}\)</span>, so that the charging time scaled inversely as <span class="mathjax-tex">\(1/\sqrt{N}\)</span>; a counterintuitive result and prohibited in classical thermodynamics. In this work, we go beyond the device design in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Quach, J. Q. et al. Superabsorption in an organic microcavity: toward a quantum battery. Sci. Adv. 8, eabk3160 (2022)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e647">38</a></sup> by incorporating a mechanism to extract stored energy from the quantum battery as an electric current.</p><p>A rich literature describes enhanced light-harvesting properties and photocurrent generation in photodiodes when embedding them inside optical microcavities<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nagarajan, K. et al. Conductivity and photoconductivity of a p-type organic semiconductor under ultrastrong coupling. ACS Nano 14, 10219–10225 (2020)." href="#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e654">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hagenmüller, D. et al. Cavity-enhanced transport of charge. Phys. Rev. Lett. 119, 223601 (2017)." href="#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e654_1">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Orgiu, E. et al. Conductivity in organic semiconductors hybridized with the vacuum field. Nat. Mater. 14, 1123–1129 (2015)." href="#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e654_2">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, B. et al. Photocurrent generation following long-range propagation of organic exciton-polaritons. Optica 9, 1029–1036 (2022)." href="#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e654_3">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Nikolis, V. C. et al. Strong light-matter coupling for reduced photon energy losses in organic photovoltaics. Nat. Commun. 10, 3706 (2019)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e657">47</a></sup>. Several mechanisms have been proposed to explain the enhanced performance of microcavity solar cells, including more efficient light trapping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Zuo, L. J. et al. Microcavity-enhanced light-trapping for highly efficient organic parallel tandem solar cells. Adv. Mater. 26, 6778–6784 (2014)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e661">48</a></sup>, the mitigation of disorder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Engelhardt, G. &amp; Cao, J. S. Unusual dynamical properties of disordered polaritons in microcavities. Phys. Rev. B 105, 064205 (2022)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e665">49</a></sup> and longer charge carrier lifetimes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Tang, Y. H. et al. Strong light-matter coupling leads to a longer charge carrier lifetime in cavity organic solar cells. ACS Photonics 11, 1627–1637 (2024)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e669">50</a></sup>. However, a comprehensive justification for microcavity enhancement remains an open question. Less attention has been given to the role of microcavity-induced collective effects on electrical energy extraction since these quantum correlations are expected to decay rapidly at room temperature. Nevertheless, these mechanisms, central to the concept of superabsorption, have the potential to offer new insights and advancements in microcavity solar cell research.</p><p>Here, we engineer a superabsorbing quantum battery that is wirelessly charged by either a coherent or an incoherent light source and outputs superextensive electrical power. The quantum battery is constructed with a multi-layered microcavity design (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig1">1a</a>) that is tuned to the resonant frequency of the ground to first-excited singlet transition of an absorber molecule, copper phthalocyanine (CuPc), to induce a strong light–matter coupling. Using ultrafast spectroscopy, we show that the charging rate scales superextensively with the number of absorber molecules. Upon charging, the energy is rapidly transferred to a metastable triplet state in CuPc, whose population persists for six orders of magnitude longer than the charging laser pulse. Electrical extraction is facilitated by charge transport layers that introduce both an energy gradient, which favours charge separation and transport, together with a charge blocking function that suppresses undesirable recombination. For a steady-state incoherent light source, the strong light–matter coupling facilitates an electrical power output that scales superextensively with the capacity of the battery. This new phenomenon is intimately related to the strong light–matter coupling underpinning the operation of the quantum battery but may find useful applications in other photovoltaic devices such as cavity-enhanced solar cells. By integrating superextensive charging, metastable energy storage, and superextensive electrical power output within a single device, our work establishes a prototypical framework for practical quantum batteries. Moreover, we report the first experimental observation of superextensive steady-state electrical discharging power, a previously unpredicted phenomenon with immediate relevance for low-light and always-charging energy storage systems enhanced by collective quantum effects.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Composition of the quantum battery tuned for strong light–matter coupling."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Composition of the quantum battery tuned for strong light–matter coupling.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41377-026-02240-6/MediaObjects/41377_2026_2240_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41377-026-02240-6/MediaObjects/41377_2026_2240_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="Fig. 1: Composition of the quantum battery tuned for strong light–matter coupling." loading="lazy" width="685" height="316"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Schematic of the layered structure of an embodiment of the quantum battery, describing the function and composition of each component. Ultrafast pump and probe laser pulses are used to charge and measure the device. <b>b</b> Work functions and HOMO/LUMO energy levels of each layer of the quantum battery, defining an energy gradient for charge separation. <b>c</b> Steady-state reflectance spectrum at 22° incidence angle for the D5 quantum battery (red line) and the optical control device (black line), which has no top silver mirror. The control spectrum is characterised by transitions to singlet states <span class="mathjax-tex">\({S}_{1}^{0}\)</span> and <span class="mathjax-tex">\({S}_{1}^{1}\)</span>. In the quantum battery, these singlet state transitions are entangled with the photonic cavity mode to give rise to polaritons, as characterised by the upper (UP), middle (MP), and lower (LP) polariton states. (<b>d</b>) and (<b>e</b>) show the reflection spectra for the D5 quantum battery and its corresponding control as a function of incidence angle</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 1" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Device engineering</h3><p>We fabricated eight quantum battery devices (labelled D1–D8) based on Fabry-Perot microcavities<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Fabry, C. Theorie et applications d’une nouvelle methods de spectroscopie intereferentielle. Ann. Chim. Ser. 7 16, 115–144 (1899)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e782">51</a></sup> whose optical length was tuned to the absorption frequency of an ensemble of CuPc molecules. The number of CuPc absorbers <span class="mathjax-tex">\(N\)</span> was adjusted in each device to span <span class="mathjax-tex">\(N\approx 2.8\times {10}^{14}\)</span> for the smallest battery to <span class="mathjax-tex">\(N\approx 7.9\times {10}^{14}\)</span> for the largest battery. To facilitate energy extraction from each device as an electric current, we incorporated a charge donor-acceptor combination of CuPc and fullerene (C<sub>60</sub>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Uchida, S. et al. Organic small molecule solar cells with a homogeneously mixed copper phthalocyanine: C60 active layer. Appl. Phys. Lett. 84, 4218–4220 (2004)." href="#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e869">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dutton, G. J. et al. Ultrafast charge-transfer processes at an oriented phthalocyanine/C60 interface. Phys. Rev. B 82, 073407 (2010)." href="#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e869_1">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Ren, J., Meng, S. &amp; Kaxiras, E. Theoretical investigation of the C60/copper phthalocyanine organic photovoltaic heterojunction. Nano Res. 5, 248–257 (2012)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e872">54</a></sup>, as well as a suitable choice of optically inert electron and hole blocking and transport materials that were integrated into the cavity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Eizner, E. et al. Organic photodiodes with an extended responsivity using ultrastrong light-matter coupling. ACS Photonics 5, 2921–2927 (2018)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e876">55</a></sup>. The schematic of the device (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig1">1a</a>) indicates the direction of laser irradiation (the detailed compositions of each device D1–D8 are shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S1</a>). For each device, we fabricated two otherwise identical structures without either the top or the bottom silver mirrors, to act as optical and electrical controls, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S2</a>). To minimise parameter variability, the controls were fabricated on the same substrate and fabrication run as the corresponding cavity devices D1–D8 (see “Materials and methods” section). Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig1">1b</a> schematically depicts the work functions and HOMO/LUMO energy levels for each layer of the cavity device. The layer composition induces an energy gradient within the cavity that facilitates charge separation of excitons created by superabsorption in the CuPc layer. Specifically, photoexcited singlet excitons (or polaritons) rapidly relax into triplet excitons, which diffuse toward the C<sub>60</sub> interface. At the interface, interfacial charge transfer states can form before eventual separation into free charges<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Dutton, G. J. et al. Ultrafast charge-transfer processes at an oriented phthalocyanine/C60 interface. Phys. Rev. B 82, 073407 (2010)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e895">53</a></sup>. The hole and electron blocking layers present a kinetic barrier for charges propagating in the backwards direction, such that a photocurrent is generated and passed between the top silver cathode and the ITO anode of the device. The layer composition was chosen to maximise the electrical performance of the quantum battery without drastically detuning the fundamental frequency of the cavity from the CuPc Q-band.</p><p>Each CuPc molecule is a three-level system with the first-excited singlet S<sub>1</sub> separated from the ground state S<sub>0</sub> by ~2 eV and a lower-lying triplet state T<sub>1</sub> at approximately 1.2 eV above S<sub>0</sub><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Dutton, G. J. &amp; Robey, S. W. Exciton dynamics at CuPc/C60 interfaces: energy dependence of exciton dissociation. J. Phys. Chem. C 116, 19173–19181 (2012)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e909">56</a></sup>. A Davydov splitting of the excited singlet arises from multipolar interactions between neighbouring chromophores, common to most phthalocyanine thin films<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Tazhibayev, S. et al. Influence of the transition metals central atom nature on the optical and electrophysical properties of phthalocyanine nanowires. Mater. Chem. Phys. 301, 127696 (2023)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e913">57</a></sup>. This feature is prominent in the reflection measurements of the optical no-cavity devices (black curve in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig1">1c</a>) and manifests as two weakly-split signals we label as <span class="mathjax-tex">\({S}_{1}^{0}\)</span> and <span class="mathjax-tex">\({S}_{1}^{1}\)</span> with approximately equal oscillator strengths. In the presence of a cavity, these absorption bands are strongly hybridised with the fundamental mode of the confined photon field leading to the formation of upper (UP), middle (MP), and lower (LP) polariton states (red curve in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig1">1c</a>). By systematically varying the number of CuPc molecules in the mixed CuPc:C<sub>60</sub> layer while keeping the cavity length fixed, we were able to tune the Rabi splitting between the polariton branches as a function of <i>N</i>. We estimate the value of <i>N</i> via stoichiometric analysis (Eq. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S3</a>) of the layer thicknesses, which are accurate to within ~1 nm. As a consequence of adjusting the CuPc concentration and layer thicknesses, the fundamental cavity frequency did not remain constant across the devices, leading to shifting asymmetries in the UP and LP reflectance bands (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S3</a>). Nevertheless, our theoretical model captured the optical response of each device very well (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S5</a>), from which it was possible to extract critical parameters of the device, such as the cavity detuning and the collective light–matter coupling. In addition, we analysed the spectral composition of the polaritonic branches of each device in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S6</a> as a function of cavity detuning.</p><p>The reflectance spectrum as a function of incidence angle for the quantum battery is shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig1">1d</a>. Overlaid are the theoretically predicted three polaritonic states. In comparison, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig1">1e</a> shows the reflectance spectrum of the no-cavity control, which is overlaid with two Davydov-split electronic states. The molecular response to an incoherent light field does not show a dependence on the angle of incidence of the light field when it is not coupled to the cavity. That the molecule-cavity coupled response (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig1">1d</a>) is weakly angle dependent is thus a signature of exciton-photon hybridisation and further evidence of exciton-polaritons.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Superextensive charging with photons</h3><p>In this section, we demonstrate the superextensive charging properties of our quantum battery devices. To reveal their dynamics, we utilise ultrafast transient spectroscopy, a technique that probes electronic and vibrational evolution in molecules and semiconductors on femtosecond time scales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Cerullo, G. et al. Time-resolved methods in biophysics. 4. Broadband pump–probe spectroscopy system with sub-20 fs temporal resolution for the study of energy transfer processes in photosynthesis. Photochem. Photobiol. Sci. 6, 135–144 (2007)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e1021">58</a></sup>. In our experiments, a laser pump pulse with bandwidth ~31 nm and resonant with the LP branch induces excitations in the quantum battery, whose time-evolution is monitored by a delayed probe pulse resonant with the UP branch (see Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S7</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S11</a>). Using this scheme, we are able to monitor the temporal dynamics of the excited-state populations (see “Materials and methods” section). When comparing the ultrafast dynamics across different devices, we maintained a constant ratio between incident photons in the laser pulse and absorber molecules in the laser volume.</p><p>The excited-state population of a representative quantum battery device is shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig2">2a</a> (orange squares), where each data point is an average of 25,000 measurements. The ultrafast differential reflectance signals for all other devices are reported in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S12</a>. Superimposed on these experimental data points is the theoretical total excited population of CuPc, which is calculated by summing the population of the first-excited singlet states (<span class="mathjax-tex">\({S}_{1}^{0},{S}_{1}^{1})\)</span> and the triplet state (<span class="mathjax-tex">\({T}_{1}\)</span>) (see “Materials and methods” section). Our ultrafast model utilised the cavity frequencies and collective couplings for each device that were derived from the steady-state reflectance experiments, thereby ensuring consistency in the theoretical description of both sets of experiments and in the derivation of the excited-state population dynamics. There is good agreement between the experimental data and the theoretically predicted populations. Using the known energy values of the singlet and triplet states<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Dutton, G. J. et al. Ultrafast charge-transfer processes at an oriented phthalocyanine/C60 interface. Phys. Rev. B 82, 073407 (2010)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e1116">53</a></sup>, we calculate the total energy of the system from their excited populations (green line in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig2">2a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Superextensive charging dynamics."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Superextensive charging dynamics.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41377-026-02240-6/MediaObjects/41377_2026_2240_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41377-026-02240-6/MediaObjects/41377_2026_2240_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="Fig. 2: Superextensive charging dynamics." loading="lazy" width="685" height="486"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> A laser pulse (pink shaded area) excites the CuPc molecules in device D5. The orange squares are the measured excited molecule populations for a representative device. (1<span class="mathjax-tex">\(\sigma\)</span> standard deviation error bars are less than the dot size). Overlaid (orange line) is the theoretical prediction, calculated by summing the first-excited singlet states (<span class="mathjax-tex">\({S}_{1}^{0},{S}_{1}^{1})\)</span> and the triplet state (<span class="mathjax-tex">\({T}_{1}\)</span>) populations, convoluted with the instrument response function. The green line gives the average total energy per molecule. <b>b</b> Table of device name, number of CuPc molecules in the laser volume <span class="mathjax-tex">\(N\)</span>, peak power density <span class="mathjax-tex">\({P}_{\max }\)</span>, charging time <span class="mathjax-tex">\({\rm{\tau }}\)</span>, and maximum energy per molecule <span class="mathjax-tex">\({E}_{\max }\)</span>. These experimentally-derived data points are plotted in (<b>c</b>, <b>d</b>, <b>e</b>) following the colour scheme in (<b>b</b>). The uncertainty in <i>N</i> is less than 8%, which is smaller than the dot size. The solid lines in (<b>c</b>, <b>d</b>, <b>e</b>) plot <i>P</i><sub><i>max</i></sub>, τ and <i>E</i><sub><i>max</i></sub> as a function of <i>N</i> for a theoretical device with cavity frequency <span class="mathjax-tex">\({\Delta }_{c}=1.87\)</span> eV and cavity loss <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\kappa =33\)</span> ps<sup>-1</sup>, respectively, which are the average values of the eight experimental devices. The power and energy density of D8 deviates significantly from the theoretical average device and is out of range for (<b>c</b> and <b>e</b>). The devices show a superextensive charging power and energy density, since these properties increase with <i>N</i>. The charging time is subextensive, as it decreases with <i>N</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 2" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>For each device, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig2">2b</a> summarises the estimated number of CuPc absorbers <i>N</i> in the pump laser cross section, the maximum stored energy per absorber (<span class="mathjax-tex">\({E}_{\max }\)</span>), the time to reach half maximum energy beginning from <span class="mathjax-tex">\(1/{e}^{2}\)</span> of the Gaussian laser pulse (τ), and the peak charging power density (<span class="mathjax-tex">\({P}_{\max }={E}_{\max }/\tau\)</span>). These values are plotted in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig2">2c–e</a>. Changes in the device composition as <i>N</i> was varied unavoidably led to slight differences in the cavity resonances and Q factors, but do not affect the overall interpretation of our results. To address whether the superextensive trends stemmed from non-linearities in the cavity resonances and Q factors rather than the collective coupling, we also plotted (solid lines in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig2">2c–e</a>) the theoretical predictions for hypothetical devices with a fixed cavity resonance (<span class="mathjax-tex">\({\Delta }_{c}=1.87\)</span> eV) and fixed photon leakage rate (<span class="mathjax-tex">\(\kappa =33\)</span> ps<sup>-1</sup>) obtained from averaging these parameters over the eight devices. Since the predictions for the hypothetical devices still show a superextensive scaling as a function of <span class="mathjax-tex">\(N\)</span>, we can conclude that non-linearities in the cavity resonances and Q factors do not dominantly contribute to the superextensive scaling observed in devices D1–D8. Due to these variances, the experimental data points deviate from the plot of a theoretical device with averaged cavity properties. In particular, D8 deviates the most from these average values, since this device exhibits the largest detuning from the Davydov resonances, explaining its relatively low <span class="mathjax-tex">\({E}_{\max }\)</span> and <span class="mathjax-tex">\({P}_{\max }\)</span> (which fall out of the range of the Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig2">2c–e</a> plots). Nevertheless, a superextensive increase in the charging power and energy density is seen, accompanied by a subextensive charging time. At this point, recall that a property such as energy density, which is classified as an intensive property according to classical thermodynamics, would remain constant with increasing <span class="mathjax-tex">\(N\)</span> in conventional energy storage devices provided that the device composition remains fixed.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Stabilisation of stored energy</h3><p>In this section, we demonstrate the metastability of the stored energy in our quantum battery devices. We measure their long-time decay dynamics using a spectrally broad supercontinuum probe with a spectral width spanning 500–1000 nm. The transient reflectance spectra for all devices are reported in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S12</a>, with a representative spectrum of D5 given in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig3">3a</a>. The positive (red) signals in the transient reflectance spectra result from ground-state bleaching, and the negative (blue) differential reflectance from excited-state absorption. Each cavity device exhibits differential reflectance signals that persist for tens of nanoseconds, showing that the excited state remains populated for six orders of magnitude longer than the device charging time (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S14</a>). These long excited-state lifetimes originate from intramolecular relaxation of the singlet excitations to metastable triplet excitations, as shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig3">3b</a>. At approximately 200 fs, intersystem crossing (ISC) becomes the dominant singlet relaxation mechanism, forming metastable triplet excitations with typical lifetimes on the order of 10–50 ns. This is three orders of magnitude longer than the equivalent state-of-the-art in microcavity quantum batteries operating at room temperature<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Quach, J. Q. et al. Superabsorption in an organic microcavity: toward a quantum battery. Sci. Adv. 8, eabk3160 (2022)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e1681">38</a></sup>. Notably, the charge retention time measured here is significantly shorter than NMR-based quantum batteries, which also operate at room temperature and have been shown to remain charged for up to two minutes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Joshi, J. &amp; Mahesh, T. S. Experimental investigation of a quantum battery using star-topology NMR spin systems. Phys. Rev. A 106, 042601 (2022)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e1685">41</a></sup>. The efficiency of this conversion mechanism is enhanced by spin-orbit coupling of the Cu<sup>2+</sup> central metal ion that works to invert the spin of the excited phthalocyanine electron<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Wang, M. F. &amp; Ishii, K. Photochemical properties of phthalocyanines with transition metal ions. Coord. Chem. Rev. 468, 214626 (2022)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e1691">59</a></sup>. Thus, the system is blocked from relaxing from the triplet <span class="mathjax-tex">\({T}_{1}\)</span> to the singlet ground state <span class="mathjax-tex">\({S}_{0}\)</span> by Pauli exclusion, resulting in energy metastabilisation in the quantum battery. In Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig3">3c</a>, we plot the long-time evolution of the excited-state CuPc populations.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Metastabilisation of stored energy via intersystem crossing."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Metastabilisation of stored energy via intersystem crossing.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41377-026-02240-6/MediaObjects/41377_2026_2240_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41377-026-02240-6/MediaObjects/41377_2026_2240_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="Fig. 3: Metastabilisation of stored energy via intersystem crossing." loading="lazy" width="685" height="611"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>After the collective excitation of the singlet states during the charging process, the energy is stored in the metastable triplet state via fast intersystem crossing (ISC). <b>a</b> Transient differential reflectivity of device D5. The persistence of the signals indicates that excited states remain populated for tens of nanoseconds, which is six orders of magnitude longer than the device charging time. <b>b</b> Energy splitting of relevant states of the CuPc absorber with dominant absorption and relaxation pathways highlighted as red arrows. The dotted line shows the spin-forbidden triplet-ground-state singlet relaxation pathway. The fluorescence quantum yield of CuPc is approximately zero due to rapid intersystem crossing <span class="mathjax-tex">\({\gamma }_{{ISC}}^{-1}=200\)</span> fs. <b>c</b> Time-evolution of the mean excited singlet (<span class="mathjax-tex">\({S}_{1}^{0}\)</span> yellow and <span class="mathjax-tex">\({S}_{1}^{1}\)</span> blue) and triplet (<span class="mathjax-tex">\({T}_{1}\)</span> purple) populations of the CuPc: within approximately 200 fs, rapid intersystem crossing converts singlet excitons into metastable triplet excitons</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 3" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Superextensive discharging of electrical power</h3><p>Controlled extraction of stored energy is a necessary feature of batteries. To power a small electronic device, for example, it is desirable for the extracted energy to be available as electrical work. Here we demonstrate the enhanced electrical energy output of our quantum batteries. In Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig4">4a, b</a>, we plot the ratio of ejected electrons to incident photons, or external quantum efficiency (EQE), for the cavity and no-cavity controls. A comparison of the two figures shows that the cavity devices have a threefold enhancement over the no-cavity controls. The polariton branches are also visible in the EQE measurements of the cavity. Despite the modest quantum efficiency enhancement observed so far, the cavity quantum battery design opens a promising avenue toward significantly improved photon-to-charge conversion, complementing and extending conventional materials-optimisation strategies.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Energy extraction from the quantum battery as electrical work."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Energy extraction from the quantum battery as electrical work.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41377-026-02240-6/MediaObjects/41377_2026_2240_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41377-026-02240-6/MediaObjects/41377_2026_2240_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="Fig. 4: Energy extraction from the quantum battery as electrical work." loading="lazy" width="685" height="621"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>A comparison of the external quantum efficiency of the (<b>a</b>) quantum batteries and their (<b>b</b>) control devices shows a threefold photon-to-charge conversion enhancement in the former. <b>c</b> Current (red lines) and discharging power (blue lines) shown as a function of voltage for a representative device D5 (solid lines) and its control (dotted lines) under constant illumination at 625 nm. <b>d</b> Plots the ratio of the peak discharging power of the quantum batteries and their control devices under constant illumination at 625 nm. This ratio increases with <i>N</i>, indicating that the discharge power of the quantum battery scales superextensively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 4" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In addition to the EQE, we investigated the discharging power of the devices (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S16</a>). Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig4">4c</a> plots current-voltage (I–V) curves for the cavity and no-cavity control of a representative device D5 (red lines), shown with their steady-state discharging power (blue lines). The optimal operating point for energy extraction is at the peak of the discharge power curve. Our devices displayed reasonable maximal discharging power densities between <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\sim 10\,\mathrm{and}\,40\,{\mathrm{\mu Wcm}}^{-2}\)</span> when compared to high-performance micro-supercapacitors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Yun, J. et al. Stretchable array of high-performance micro-supercapacitors charged with solar cells for wireless powering of an integrated strain sensor. Nano Energy 49, 644–654 (2018)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e1990">60</a></sup>, reported to show power densities of <span class="mathjax-tex">\(30.2\,\mathrm{and}\,176.5\,{\mathrm{\mu Wcm}}^{-2}\)</span>. We investigated the ratio of cavity to no-cavity peak discharging power, <span class="mathjax-tex">\({P}_{{\rm{c}}}^{\max }/{P}_{{\rm{nc}}}^{\max }\)</span>; as the cavity and no-cavity controls were fabricated on the same substrate, this ratio accounts for variations between fabrication runs and for the change in carrier dynamics of CuPc due to differing concentrations of C<sub>60</sub> across the devices. In Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig4">4d</a>, we observe a linear increase in <span class="mathjax-tex">\({P}_{{\rm{c}}}^{\max }/{P}_{{\rm{nc}}}^{\max }\)</span> with <span class="mathjax-tex">\(N\)</span>, indicating superextensive discharging power in the cavity quantum battery devices. In comparison, we point out that a constant discharging power ratio greater than one and independent of <i>N</i> would indicate a non-superextensive enhancement. This superextensive discharging power has not previously been reported and suggests a collective enhancement stemming from the strong light–matter coupling in the quantum batteries.</p><p>The microscopic origin of superextensive electrical discharging arises from the polaritonic dressing of transitions to the excited singlet states of CuPc induced by the microcavity. As the energy of the dressed state scales with the number of absorbers as <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\sqrt{N}\)</span>, the open-circuit voltage also grows according to <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\sqrt{N}\)</span> (see Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S17</a>). This allows the photocurrent to persist to higher applied voltages, resulting in a maximum discharging power that scales as <span class="mathjax-tex">\({P}_{c}^{\max }\sim {N}^{2}\)</span>. Using a novel quantum battery model that accounts for charge transfer between redox states of the chromophores (see Supplementary Section <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">4</a>), we identified a crossover between linear and superlinear regimes of electrical discharging power that occurs precisely when the quantum battery transitions into the strong-coupling regime (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S19</a>). Techniques such as multidimensional coherent spectroscopy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Muir, J. B. et al. Interactions between Fermi polarons in monolayer WS2. Nat. Commun. 13, 6164 (2022)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e2260">61</a></sup> and ultrafast photocurrent measurements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Ma, Q. et al. Photocurrent as a multiphysics diagnostic of quantum materials. Nat. Rev. Phys. 5, 170–184 (2023)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e2264">62</a></sup> may offer further insights into the microscopic mechanisms driving this enhancement.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>Our results reveal that strong light–matter coupling not only facilitates superextensive charging dynamics in microcavity quantum batteries but also enables superextensive electrical discharging in the steady-state—a regime typically unexplored for quantum enhancements based on collective effects. This behaviour emerges from the interplay between rapid superabsorption, moderated by dephasing mechanisms that suppress superradiant decay, and the efficient conversion of excitons into free charge carriers via intersystem crossing. Crucially, this cascade supports a steady-state light-to-charge conversion process whose power output scales super-linearly with system size, even under low-intensity, incoherent illumination. This constitutes the first experimental realisation of steady-state superextensive electrical power, marking a significant step toward practical quantum-enhanced energy harvesting.</p><p>We have studied the operation of our devices at room temperature under ambient conditions as the most applicable regime for future quantum battery technologies. Temperature-dependent electrical discharge studies offer a further avenue to probe the influence of thermal noise on the superextensive light-to-charge conversion mechanism. The role of hybrid charge-phonon polaron states in charge transport may also be further elucidated by temperature-dependent discharging experiments in which the contributions from lattice vibrations may be quenched at low temperatures. Future studies could employ time-resolved XPS measurements as a direct probe of the charge separation in CuPc:C<sub>60</sub> complexes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Roth, F. et al. Direct observation of charge separation in an organic light harvesting system by femtosecond time-resolved XPS. Nat. Commun. 12, 1196 (2021)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e2282">63</a></sup>. Additionally, this technique may be exploited to study superextensive photocurrents that stem from polaron-polariton quantum states<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Krainova, N. et al. Polaron photoconductivity in the weak and strong light-matter coupling regime. Phys. Rev. Lett. 124, 177401 (2020)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e2286">64</a></sup>.</p><p>Until now, the study of quantum batteries has been predominantly a theoretical endeavour with scarce experimental verification. As the first experimental demonstration of a full operational cycle of a quantum battery (from superabsorption and energy metastabilisation of light energy to superextensive energy extraction as an electric current), our device represents a decisive step forward in the development of quantum battery technologies and offers an intriguing platform for the exploration of foundational photophysics at the frontier of ultrafast light-to-charge conversion and quantum thermodynamics.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Device fabrication</h3><p>ITO-coated glass substrates (Asahi Glass Co. Ltd., Japan) were cleaned with isopropyl alcohol and deionised water in an ultrasonic bath. The substrates were then subjected to a UV−ozone treatment in a UV−ozone cleaner (Novascan PSD Pro) to obtain an oxygen-rich ITO surface, increasing the work function of ITO. A 75 nm thick Ag (Sigma-Aldrich) bottom mirror layer was then deposited on the ITO layer, followed by a 15 nm hole-injection HAT-CN (Luminescence Technology Co.) layer under high vacuum. From then on, all layers up to the Ag top mirror layer were deposited using a thermal evaporation method under high vacuum (&lt;1.0 × 10<sup>−4 </sup>Pa). Next, a 15 nm CuPc layer, a mixed layer of CuPc:C<sub>60</sub> (Luminescence Technology Co.), and a C<sub>60</sub> layer were sequentially deposited (the thicknesses of these layers varied for each device). A 15 nm BPhen (Luminescence Technology Co.) and a 1 nm LiF (Sigma-Aldrich) layer were then deposited as electron transport and injection layers, respectively. Finally, a 25 nm Ag (Sigma-Aldrich Solutions) top mirror layer was deposited on the LiF layer. The devices were encapsulated with cover glass and a desiccant, and then sealed with a UV-curable epoxy resin. The final device is ~0.5 × 2 cm<sup>2</sup> and has four separated 2 × 5 mm<sup>2</sup> regions of cavity, and two 2 × 5 mm<sup>2</sup> regions of the corresponding no-cavity controls. Further fabrication details can be found in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec10">Steady-state spectroscopy</h3><p>Angle–resolved reflectometry measurements were performed with an Agilent Cary 7000 UV-Visible-NIR spectrophotometer with Universal Measurement Accessory (UMA) and xenon lamp source. Measurements across the devices were baseline-corrected and performed with standardised spot size and lamp intensity. Fixed-angle reflectometry and transmission measurements were performed with an Agilent Cary 5000 UV-Visible-NIR spectrophotometer with Diffuse Reflectance Accessory (DRA) and xenon lamp source. Measurements across the devices were baseline-corrected and performed with standardised spot size and lamp intensity. Reflectance measurements were collected at 8° angle of incidence to enable the collection of a specular and diffuse reflectance signal.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec11">Ultrafast spectroscopy</h3><p>Femtosecond transient reflection spectroscopy was used to probe the depletion of a common ground state between the LP and UP. We used two-colour pump-probe spectroscopy to circumvent pump scatter, which can obscure degenerate pump-probe measurements. The pump-probe measurement produces a differential reflectivity</p><div id="Equa" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$\frac{\Delta R}{R}=\frac{{R}_{{ON}}-{R}_{{OFF}}}{{R}_{{OFF}}}$$</span></div></div><p>where <span class="mathjax-tex">\({R}_{{ON}}\)</span> (<span class="mathjax-tex">\({R}_{{OFF}}\)</span>) is the probe reflectivity with (without) a pump pulse. Since an increase in probe reflectivity in response to the pump is associated with the quantum system absorbing energy (i.e., by inducing a resonant transition <span class="mathjax-tex">\(i\to f\)</span> in the quantum battery), <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\Delta R/R\)</span> is proportional to the population <span class="mathjax-tex">\({P}_{i}\)</span> of the initial state <span class="mathjax-tex">\(i\)</span><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Quach, J. Q. et al. Superabsorption in an organic microcavity: toward a quantum battery. Sci.
