Adv. 8, eabk3160 (2022)." href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d290388553e2545">38</a></sup>. A probe wavelength resonant with the ground to upper polariton transition was used, such that an increase in differential reflectivity provides a time-resolved measure of the ground-state population or, conversely, of the sum of all excited-state populations. We pump and probe at the lower and upper polaritons, respectively, specifically not to trigger relaxation dynamics between the polariton branches during the time-evolution of the experiment.</p><p>Both two-colour pump-probe and pump-supercontinuum-probe measurements were performed on the quantum battery devices in reflection geometry, at room temperature, in air. The two-colour femtosecond laser pulses were generated by two NOPAs (Light Conversion, Orpheus-N-2H and Orpheus-N-3H). The NOPAs were pumped by a Yb:KGW laser amplifier (Light Conversion Pharos, 1030 nm, 180 fs, 100 µJ pulses). A secondary identical amplifier is seeded by the oscillator of the first amplifier (Pharos Duo, Light Conversion). The output of the secondary amplifier was focused onto a 2 mm sapphire crystal to generate a broadband supercontinuum which spanned 500–1000 nm. Amplifier pulses were selected at a repetition rate of 33.3 kHz for all measurements. The two-colour pump-probe was used to measure the sub-100 fs dynamics, while the pump-supercontinuum-probe was used to measure spectrally broad long-time dynamics.</p><p>We determined and optimised the pulse durations using auto- and cross-correlation measurements (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S7</a>). The pump pulses from the 2H-NOPA had an average pulse width (Gaussian FWHM) of 34 ± 1 fs. The probe pulses from the 3H-NOPA had an average pulse width of 32 ± 3 fs across all measurements. We used the measured cross-correlation widths (~50 fs) for the instrument response function in the theoretical model. The pump and probe beams were focused onto the QB devices with an almost collinear geometry (~4° between beam paths) with a FWHM spot diameter of 32 µm for the pump, and 42 µm for the probe. We have checked that the relative sizing between the pump and probe does not change the dynamics of the <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\Delta R/R\)</span> signal. The QB devices were rotated 25° to the incoming pump and probe pulses so the reflected probe beam could be spatially isolated and re-collimated. The probe was then directed into a spectrometer with a high-speed CCD (Entwicklungsbuero Stresing, FLC3030) triggered by the laser amplifier. To measure the <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\Delta R/R\)</span> signal, the pump was modulated by a mechanical chopper operating at 16.67 kHz (SciTec Instruments, 310CD). To scan the <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\Delta R/R\)</span> signal as a function of time, the probe beam was sent through optical retroreflector delay lines, which extended to 8 ns for the supercontinuum probe pulses (Newport, DL325) or to 0.8 ns for the NOPA probe pulses (Newport, ESP300). Longer supercontinuum probe delay times out to ~16 μs were achieved by electronically triggering the secondary amplifier from later oscillator pulses (period of 13.3 ns) relative to the first amplifier. After each pump pulse is delivered to the devices, they show a <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\Delta R/R\)</span> signal out to ~10 ns (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">S10</a>), and the next pump pulse arrives ~66 μs later.</p><p>Since the chopper modulates at half the repetition rate of the pump laser, sequential probe-only and pump-probe pulses are incident on the devices, thus enabling the acquisition of shot-to-shot <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\Delta R/R\)</span>. The two-colour pump-probe measurements were scanned over the probe delay of −5 to 10 ps, with −200 to 400 fs in steps of 10 fs. At each probe delay time, 5000 shot-to-shot <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\Delta R/R\)</span> spectra are averaged. Measurements across the entire probe delay were repeated 5 times under identical experimental conditions, and the average of these measurements was taken, with the 1σ standard deviation as the error bars.</p><p>To compare the ultrafast dynamics across different devices, it was important to keep a constant ratio of incident photons to absorber molecules in the laser volume. To achieve this, the fluence and wavelength of the pump-probe pulses were varied to account for the steady-state reflection spectrum of each device (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a>). The fluence of the pump as a function of <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\Delta R/R\)</span> intensity followed a linear dependence for all fluences used in the experiments (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a>), ensuring higher-order effects induced by the pump are negligible in the measurements.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">External quantum efficiency measurements</h3><p>External quantum efficiency of the devices was calculated as the ratio of the number of photons absorbed by the device to the number of photons that participate in generating a photocurrent. This is given by <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\hslash \omega I/{eP}\)</span>, where <span class="mathjax-tex">\(I\)</span> is the photocurrent, <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\hslash \omega\)</span> is the photon energy, <span class="mathjax-tex">\(P\)</span> is the power of incident photons, and <span class="mathjax-tex">\(e\)</span> is the electron charge.</p><p>External quantum efficiency spectroscopy was performed at the <i>RMIT Nanostructures Laboratory</i> using a PV Measurements QEXL quantum efficiency instrument with Xenon arc lamp and monochromator, calibrated with a Si photodiode at 300–1100 nm. Internal quantum efficiency was calculated by dividing the experimentally measured external quantum efficiency by the absorption at each wavelength, obtained by fixed-angle UV-Visible-NIR reflectance and transmission spectroscopy on an Agilent Cary 5000 UV-Vis-NIR spectrophotometer with DRA and xenon arc lamp.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Discharging power measurements</h3><p>I-V measurements were collected at the <i>RMIT Nanostructures Laboratory</i> using a Keithley 2636B SYSTEM SourceMeter® and a collimated 625 nm LED source (ThorLabs M625L4) at 10 mW/cm<sup>2</sup>, with a resolution of 0.01 V. Measurements proceeded by first illuminating the device with the LED source, then varying the bias voltage from 1.5 V to −1.5 V. The power of each device was calculated at the point where the product of current and voltage is at a maximum, i.e., the maximum power point, according to P = IV.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Theoretical model</h3><p>The reflectance spectra were modelled using a coupled oscillator Hamiltonian</p><div id="Equ2" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${H}_{\text{CO}}={\Delta }_{c}{a}^{\dagger }a+{\Delta }_{1}{X}_{11}+{\Delta }_{2}{X}_{22}+\,{g}_{{co}}\left({a}^{\dagger }{X}_{01}+{X}_{10}a+{a}^{\dagger }{X}_{02}+{X}_{20}a\right)$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (1)
                </div></div><p>where a<sup>(†)</sup> annihilates (creates) excitations of the confined photon field with frequency Δ<sub>c</sub> and X<sub><i>αβ</i></sub> = |α〉〈β| are Hilbert operators that work on the low-lying electronic levels of the CuPc absorber. The indices <span class="mathjax-tex">\({\rm{\alpha }},{\rm{\beta }}\in \left\{\mathrm{0,1,2}\right\}\)</span> enumerate the ground <span class="mathjax-tex">\({S}_{0}\)</span>, first-excited <span class="mathjax-tex">\({S}_{1}^{0}\)</span> and second-excited <span class="mathjax-tex">\({S}_{1}^{1}\)</span> levels, respectively, with energies <span class="mathjax-tex">\(0\)</span>, <span class="mathjax-tex">\({\Delta }_{1}\)</span> and <span class="mathjax-tex">\({\Delta }_{2}\)</span> as in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Fig3">3b</a>. Since we do not probe the steady-state reflectance of our devices in the near infra-red, the triplet state dynamics do not contribute to the simulated reflectance, thus we do not consider their population in our coupled oscillator model. The collective light–matter coupling constant <span class="mathjax-tex">\({g}_{{co}}\)</span> quantifies the interaction of the CuPc ensemble electric dipole with the quantised cavity radiation field and is related to the bare coupling <span class="mathjax-tex">\(g\)</span> via <span class="mathjax-tex">\({g}_{{co}}=\sqrt{N}g\)</span>. The steady-state reflectance of the cavity was simulated within a Fermi golden rule approach</p><div id="Equ3" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$R\approx 1-A=1-{I}_{0}\mathop{\sum }\limits_{{\rm{\mu }}}{\left|\left\langle {\phi }_{{\rm{\mu }}}\left|\left.{a}^{\dagger }\right|\right.{\phi }_{0}\right\rangle \right|}^{2}{e}^{-{\left({{\rm{\epsilon }}}_{{\rm{\mu }}}-{{\rm{\epsilon }}}_{0}-{\rm{\nu }}\right)}^{2}/2{\rm{\sigma }}}$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (2)
                </div></div><p>involving the coupled oscillator eigenstates |ϕ<sub>μ</sub>〉 with corresponding energies <span class="mathjax-tex">\({{\rm{\epsilon }}}_{{\rm{\mu }}}\)</span> that depend upon the microscopic parameters of the Hamiltonian (1). The intensity <span class="mathjax-tex">\({I}_{0}\)</span> and combined homogeneous and inhomogeneous broadening <span class="mathjax-tex">\({\rm{\sigma }}\)</span> are fitting parameters, and <span class="mathjax-tex">\({\rm{\nu }}\)</span> is the frequency of incident photons external to the quantum battery.</p><p>The pertinent dynamics of the ultrafast charging experiments are captured by the Tavis–Cummings Hamiltonian</p><div id="Equ4" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$\begin{array}{l}H\left(t\right)=({\Delta}_{c}-{\rm{\nu }}){a}^{\dagger}a+i{\rm{\eta }}\left(t\right)\left({a}^{\dagger }-a\right)+\mathop{\sum }\limits_{j=1}^{N}\left(({\Delta}_{1}-{\rm{\nu }}){X}_{11}^{\left(j\right)}+({\Delta}_{2}-{\rm{\nu }}){X}_{22}^{\left(j\right)}+{\Delta }_{T}{X}_{TT}^{\left(j\right)}\right)\\ +\,\,g\mathop{\sum }\limits_{j=1}^{N}\left({a}^{\dagger }{X}_{01}^{\left(j\right)}+{X}_{10}^{\left(j\right)}a+{a}^{\dagger }{X}_{02}^{\left(j\right)}+{X}_{20}^{\left(j\right)}a\right)\end{array}$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (3)
                </div></div><p>written in the rotating frame of the laser. We assume a Gaussian temporal profile <span class="mathjax-tex">\({\rm{\eta }}\left(t\right)=\exp \left[-{\left(\left(t-{t}_{0}\right)/2{\rm{\sigma }}\right)}^{2}\right]/{\rm{\sigma }}\sqrt{2{\rm{\pi }}}\)</span> for the pulse envelope and FWHM of <span class="mathjax-tex">\(34\)</span> fs. The dissipative interactions between the cavity quantum battery and its environment are accounted for with the Lindblad master equation</p><div id="Equ5" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$\begin{array}{l}\dot{{{\rho }}}=-i\left[H\left(t\right),{{\rho }}\right]+k{\mathcal{L}}\left[a\right]+\mathop{\sum }\limits_{j=1}^{N}\left({\gamma }^{-}\left({\mathcal{L}}\left[{X}_{01}^{\left(j\right)}\right]+\,{\mathcal{L}}\left[{X}_{02}^{\left(j\right)}\right]\right)+{\gamma }_{T}^{-}{\mathcal{L}}\left[{X}_{0T}^{\left(j\right)}\right]\right.\\ +\,\left.{\gamma }^{z}\left({\mathcal{L}}\left[{X}_{11}^{\left(j\right)}\right]+{\mathcal{L}}\left[{X}_{22}^{\left(j\right)}\right]\right)+{{\rm{\gamma }}}_{\text{ISC}}\left({\mathcal{L}}\left[{X}_{T1}^{\left(j\right)}\right]+{\mathcal{L}}\left[{X}_{T2}^{\left(j\right)}\right]\right)\right)\end{array}$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (4)
                </div></div><p>where <span class="mathjax-tex">\({\mathcal{L}}\left[O\right]=O{\rm{\rho }}{O}^{\dagger }-\frac{1}{2}\left\{{O}^{\dagger }O,{\rm{\rho }}\right\}\)</span> are Lindblad jump operators. In Eq. (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41377-026-02240-6#Equ5">4</a>), we account for the rate of photon loss from the cavity <span class="mathjax-tex">\({\rm{\kappa }}\)</span>, the rate of non-radiative relaxation from the singlet <span class="mathjax-tex">\({\gamma }^{-}\)</span> and triplet <span class="mathjax-tex">\({\gamma }_{T}^{-}\)</span> states, intersystem crossing <span class="mathjax-tex">\({{\rm{\gamma }}}_{\text{ISC}}\)</span> and singlet dephasing <span class="mathjax-tex">\({\gamma }^{z}\)</span>.</p><p>The ultrafast transient reflectance experiments provide a measure of the summed excited-state CuPc populations <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\Delta R/R\propto \left\langle {X}_{{TT}}\right\rangle +\left\langle {X}_{11}\right\rangle +\left\langle {X}_{22}\right\rangle\)</span>. The populations <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\left\langle {X}_{{\rm{\alpha }}{\rm{\alpha }}}\right\rangle\)</span> for a molecule in the ensemble are obtained by numerically solving a hierarchical set of coupled equations of motion for the molecular and cavity degrees of freedom, which, in general, do not close. We enforce the closure of these equations by approximating the time-evolution of three-body correlators as <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\left\langle {ABC}\right\rangle =\left\langle {AB}\right\rangle \left\langle C\right\rangle +\left\langle {AC}\right\rangle \left\langle B\right\rangle +\left\langle A\right\rangle \left\langle {BC}\right\rangle -2\left\langle A\right\rangle \left\langle B\right\rangle \left\langle C\right\rangle\)</span>, thus capturing the pertinent dynamics with corrections scaling as <span class="mathjax-tex">\(O(1/N)\)</span>. By solving for the populations 〈X<sub>αα</sub>〉, we obtain the time-evolution of the quantum battery energy density <span class="mathjax-tex">\(E\left(t\right)={\rm{\hslash }}\left({\Delta }_{1}\left\langle {X}_{11}\right\rangle +{\Delta }_{2}\left\langle {X}_{22}\right\rangle +{\Delta }_{T}\left\langle {X}_{{TT}}\right\rangle \right)\)</span>, the charging time <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\tau\)</span> (defined by <span class="mathjax-tex">\(E\left(\tau \right)={E}_{\max }/2\)</span>) and the maximum charging power <span class="mathjax-tex">\({P}_{\max }={E}_{\max }/\tau\)</span>, where <span class="mathjax-tex">\({E}_{\max }\)</span> is the maximum energy density stored in the device.</p></div></div></section>
                    
                </div>

              <p>The data that support the findings of this study are available from the corresponding author on request.</p>
